    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="2eceb332b71b4e4db5b241c8e1eb22d8">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '19292dd4-d482-4c11-bca3-598bd16f587c'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Ni/Youxuan">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Lei/Kaixiang" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a3" data-db-target-for="a3" aria-controls="a3" aria-haspopup="true" id="a3_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Kaixiang Lei</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a3" aria-labelledby="a3_Ctrl" role="region" id="a3">
                     <p class="author-name">Kaixiang Lei</p>
                     <p>Key Laboratory of Advanced Energy Materials Chemistry (Ministry of Education), College of Chemistry, Nankai University, Tianjin, 300071 P. R. China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Lei/Kaixiang">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Dong/Huanhuan" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a4" data-db-target-for="a4" aria-controls="a4" aria-haspopup="true" id="a4_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Huanhuan Dong</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a4" aria-labelledby="a4_Ctrl" role="region" id="a4">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d30742908" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p><b>Give us a tune</b>: 3D porous Sn on Cu foil (Sn/Cu) is prepared by tuning the redox potential of Cu<sup>+</sup>/Cu with a ligand of thiourea to trigger the replacement reaction between Cu and Sn<sup>2+</sup>. The resultant full battery of Sn/Cu//Na<sub>3</sub>(VO<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>F<sub>2</sub> delivers a high energy density of 311.7 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> with a long lifespan of 200 cycles.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6b08d8dc-6752-47a3-a15a-cefaacb5b961/cssc201801662-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6b08d8dc-6752-47a3-a15a-cefaacb5b961/cssc201801662-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1cc0c885-d04c-4ca3-8f3c-ac4fa1cf0caf/cssc201801662-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6b08d8dc-6752-47a3-a15a-cefaacb5b961/cssc201801662-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d30742911" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted significant research interest for large-scale electric energy storage. However, anodes with good rate capability and long cycle life are still lacking. Here, a three-dimensional (3D) porous Sn on Cu foil (Sn/Cu) is prepared by tuning the redox potential of Cu<sup>+</sup>/Cu with a ligand of thiourea to trigger the replacement reaction between Cu and Sn<sup>2+</sup>. The as-synthesized Sn/Cu is used as an integrated porous electrode and can be directly applied as an advanced freestanding electrode for SIBs. Such a unique structure can efficiently relieve the strain caused by sodiation/desodiation and benefit penetration of the electrolyte and diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>. This is a merit of its large reversible capacity of about 700 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2500 mA g<sup>−1</sup> for 400 cycles. A full battery of Sn/Cu//Na<sub>3</sub>(VO<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>F<sub>2</sub> is constructed, which presents a high energy density of 311.7 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and long lifespan of 200 cycles. This facile synthesis strategy and good electrochemical performance will encourage more investigations into structure design of functional materials.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cssc201801662-sec-0001">
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted extensive research interests owing to the low cost, abundance, and wide distribution of Na resources.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0001_R_d30742899e360" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></span> However, the large ionic radius and heavy atomic weight of Na (1.02 Å, 22.99 g mol<sup>−1</sup>) lead to sluggish kinetics and low storage capacity.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0002_R_d30742899e364" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></span> Severe damage to the electrode structure during cycling results in decaying performance over multiple cycles, especially for anodes. Various strategies, such as soft/hard templates and solvo/hydrothermal methods, have been applied to synthesize hollow (or porous) materials.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0003_R_d30742899e366" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></span> They can partially accommodate the volume change incurred by Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction, and promote the electrochemical performance. Nevertheless, they are not favorable for large-scale production. Therefore, rational design and synthesis of micro/nanostructured anode materials on a large scale are needed for facile Na storage in SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Metal anodes based on alloying/dealloying reactions have received much attention.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0004_R_d30742899e373" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></span> Typically, Sn presents a large theoretical capacity of 847 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for formation of Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0005_R_d30742899e381" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></span> However, the volume change of Sn during sodiation/desodiation is 420 %, which induces particle pulverization and cracks in electrode pellets, resulting in performance decay.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0005a" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0005a_R_d30742899e384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5a - J. Electrochem. Soc.">5a</a></span> To relieve the strain caused by Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction, Sn nanoparticles have been attempted. They were embedded into carbon matrix, such as small Sn nanoparticles in a spherical carbon network, Sn supported on carbon nanotubes, and Sn/N-doped carbon composites, etc.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0006_R_d30742899e388" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></span> Although these can reduce the effect of volume change of Sn on its electrochemical performance, the applied carbon in the composite compromises the large capacity of Sn. Moreover, the tedious synthetic procedure does not favor large-scale production. It was recently reported that a one-step replacement reaction was used to synthesize Bi nanoflakes on a three-dimensional (3D) porous Ni foam to boost the electrochemical storage of Na, which largely simplifies the production of electrodes.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0007_R_d30742899e390" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a></span> Because of the low melting point and softness of Sn, it will be quite challenging to construct 3D porous Sn electrodes on a large scale.</p>
         
         <p>Owing to the more positive redox potential of either Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Cu (0.342 V) or Cu<sup>+</sup>/Cu (0.521 V) than that of Sn<sup>2+</sup>/Sn (−0.137 V),<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0008_R_d30742899e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></span> the replacement of Sn<sup>2+</sup> by Cu is thermodynamically unfavorable. Herein, we report that, by introducing a ligand of thiourea, the redox potential of {Cu[CS(NH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>]<sub>4</sub>}<sup>+</sup>/Cu is reduced to −0.391 V and ensures the replacement reaction between Sn<sup>2+</sup> and Cu. With the aid of sodium dodecyl sulfate (SDS), a 3D porous Sn on Cu foil (Sn/Cu) is in situ produced at room temperature. This process can be facilely scaled up, and the as-synthesized Sn/Cu can be directly employed as anodes for SIBs. It shows remarkable cycling stability and rate capability. The full battery with a configuration of Sn/Cu//Na<sub>3</sub>(VO<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>F<sub>2</sub> (Sn/Cu//NVPF) delivers a high energy density of 311.7 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> based on the total mass of both electrodes. This facile synthetic route enables the replacement of Sn<sup>2+</sup> by Cu, and will trigger more attention for the rational synthesis of nanostructured materials.</p>
         
         <p>Thiourea is a planar molecule with the formula SC(NH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, where the two lone pairs of electrons in the S atom make it a strong ligand to coordinate with cations and change the chemical environment.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0009_R_d30742899e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></span> As predicted by density functional theory (DFT) calculations, each Cu<sup>+</sup> can be coordinated with four thiourea molecules to give a stable complex of {Cu[SC(NH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>]<sub>4</sub>}<sup>+</sup> (Figure S1 in the Supporting Information). Each S atom of thiourea shares 0.27 of negative charge, and the Cu atom has 0.15 of positive charge, leading to strong coordination bonding between Cu and S with bond lengths of 2.35 Å and a high stability constant of 10<sup>15.4</sup>.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0010_R_d30742899e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></span> This results in a much more negative equilibrium potential of −0.391 V for {Cu[SC(NH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>]<sub>4</sub>}<sup>+</sup>/Cu, as calculated by the Nernst equation (in the Supporting Information), than 0.521 V for Cu<sup>+</sup>/Cu. Experimentally, the open-circuit potential of the Cu electrode is tuned to −1.121 V (vs. Hg/Hg<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>, sat. K<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>) in 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> solution containing 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> thiourea, and lower than that without thiourea (−0.491 V) in Figure S2 (in the Supporting Information), which is consistent with the calculation. Although the replacement of Cu by Sn<sup>2+</sup> is not thermodynamically favored, with the addition of thiourea it occurs spontaneously in aqueous solution at room temperature. The synthesis process is schematically illustrated in Scheme <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-5001">1</a>, and can be facilely scaled up.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cssc201801662-fig-5001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fc282ee0-072f-4c79-a978-10cd2c272432/cssc201801662-fig-5001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/fc282ee0-072f-4c79-a978-10cd2c272432/cssc201801662-fig-5001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2e6f06e1-4919-4f89-bc31-9b20f073675c/cssc201801662-fig-5001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fc282ee0-072f-4c79-a978-10cd2c272432/cssc201801662-fig-5001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc201801662-fig-5001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.201801662" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Illustration of synthesis of Sn/Cu and replacement reaction of Cu and Sn<sup>2+</sup> with the aid of thiourea.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of the resultant Sn/Cu is shown in Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0001">1</a> a, which suggests formation of Sn by the replacement of Cu in the foil. The residual minor peak at 30° is ascribed to the copper–tin intermetallic phase (Cu<sub>6</sub>Sn<sub>5</sub>).<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0011_R_d30742899e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Surf. Coat. Technol.">11</a></span> The insets of Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0001">1</a> a present the color contrast between Sn/Cu and the pristine Cu foil. The silver-white Sn is selectively produced on one side of the Cu foil and preserves good flexibility. To control the microstructure of Sn on the Cu foil, different surfactants were added in the synthesis process, as depicted in Figures S3 and S4 (in the Supporting Information).<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0012_R_d30742899e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a></span> Anionic surfactants, such as sodium dodecyl sulfate (SDS) and sodium dodecyl benzene sulfonate (SDBS), enable porous Sn networks (Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0001">1</a> b, Figures S5 and S6 in the Supporting Information). The porous structure of Sn is gradually formed, and the SDS favors more uniform pore generation, as shown in Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0001">1</a> b at a reaction time of 3 h. For comparison, the Sn layer is smooth or non-uniform with addition of hexadecyl trimethyl ammonium bromide (CTAB) or polyvinyl pyrrolidone (PVP) into reaction solutions (Figures S7–S9 in the Supporting Information). The Sn/Cu was examined by atomic force microscope (AFM) (Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0001">1</a> c). Its porous nature is clear and consistent with that in Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0001">1</a> b. Moreover, energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) mapping and the spectrum of the cross section of the selected area in Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0001">1</a> d indicate uniform distribution of Sn on the Cu foil with a thickness of approximately 6 μm, which is much thicker than that without any surfactants (Figure S10 in the Supporting Information). The thickness of the Sn layer is increased with the reaction time (Figure S11 in the Supporting Information). With the aid of an anionic surfactant and thiourea, porous Sn/Cu can be synthesized by a facile replacement reaction.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cssc201801662-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/01dad3b3-5d1f-47d3-82ca-793cb380fd04/cssc201801662-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/01dad3b3-5d1f-47d3-82ca-793cb380fd04/cssc201801662-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c7d630d7-7234-49eb-9715-89a33e12602e/cssc201801662-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/01dad3b3-5d1f-47d3-82ca-793cb380fd04/cssc201801662-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc201801662-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.201801662" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) XRD pattern of the as-synthesized Sn/Cu with insets of photos. (b) SEM images of the top view of Sn/Cu at different magnifications. (c) AFM image of Sn/Cu. (d) SEM image and EDS mapping and spectrum of the cross section of the selected area.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To understand the growth process of Sn on a Cu foil, cross sections of the Sn/Cu were prepared by focused ion beam (FIB), and were captured by transmission electron microscopy (TEM; Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0002">2</a> a). Sn and Cu are apparently observed with an interface as a buffer layer between them. In high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images of Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0002">2</a> a, well-developed lattices with d-spacings of 0.20 nm of Cu correspond to its (1 1 1) facets. The phase of the interface is identified as monoclinic Cu<sub>6</sub>Sn<sub>5</sub> by the characteristic fast Fourier transform (FFT) diffraction pattern in Figure S12 (in the Supporting Information), consistent with the XRD pattern in Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0001">1</a> a. The neighboring facets with d-spacings of 0.29 and 0.27 nm correspond to the (3 1 1) and (1 1 3) planes, respectively, and their angle is 49.9°, further confirming formation of Cu<sub>6</sub>Sn<sub>5</sub>. It is approximately 15.8 nm in thickness. The d-spacing of 0.28 nm in the HRTEM image of Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0002">2</a> a matches well with the (1 0 1) facet of Sn, as well as the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern in Figure S12 (in the Supporting Information). It is clear that the interface layer of Cu<sub>6</sub>Sn<sub>5</sub> promotes the formation of porous Sn on the Cu foil.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cssc201801662-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/acbc781f-00fd-4a0e-982d-bfb5fcc81e03/cssc201801662-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/acbc781f-00fd-4a0e-982d-bfb5fcc81e03/cssc201801662-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/565779d6-3be0-4fb1-b60b-af6b53121877/cssc201801662-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/acbc781f-00fd-4a0e-982d-bfb5fcc81e03/cssc201801662-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc201801662-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.201801662" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) HRTEM images and EDS mappings of the cross section of Sn/Cu. (b) Schematic diagram of the proposed formation mechanism of 3D porous Sn on Cu foil.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The reaction mechanism of the replacement between Cu and Sn<sup>2+</sup> is proposed as schematically depicted in Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0002">2</a> b. Thiourea traps Cu<sup>+</sup> through strong Cu−S bonds to form a complex of {Cu[SC(NH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>]<sub>4</sub>}<sup>+</sup> (Figure S1 in the Supporting Information), and promotes the replacement reaction of Cu by Sn<sup>2+</sup>. Owing to the large difference between the facets of face-centered cubic Cu and tetragonal Sn, Sn is firstly replaced to alloy with Cu and form a thin Cu<sub>6</sub>Sn<sub>5</sub> interlayer (Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0002">2</a> a).<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0013_R_d30742899e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Mater. Lett.">13</a></span> Then, pure Sn is continuously produced to build a 3D porous structure with the assistance of SDS. SDS has a hydrophobic long chain [C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>25</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>]<sup>−</sup> in aqueous solution. Its negative headgroup can electrostatically interact with Sn<sup>2+</sup> and assemble in solution, in which the Sn<sup>2+</sup> can only be transported to the Cu surface between the hydrophobic long chains to control the microstructure of Sn.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0014_R_d30742899e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Mater. Sci. Mater. Electron.">14</a></span> In this process, the surfactant SDS acts like a pore-forming agent, and promotes formation of 3D porous Sn on a Cu foil.</p>
         
         <p>The as-synthesized Sn/Cu was cut into electrode pellets with a diameter of 12 mm, which were assembled against Na foils in coin cells with 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DEGDME) as the electrolyte. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves were recorded to evaluate the electrochemical behavior of the Sn/Cu electrode. As shown in Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0003">3</a> a, three typical reduction peaks at 0.20, 0.11, and 0.02 V can be observed in the cathodic scans, and the following three oxidation peaks at 0.24, 0.55, and 0.66 V are attributed to gradual desodiation, indicative of good reversibility. Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) profiles of the Sn/Cu electrode at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> are shown in Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0003">3</a> b. It is clear that there exist four distinctive charge plateaus, which are ascribed to desodiation of Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>, Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>, NaSn, and NaSn<sub>3</sub>, respectively, based on the respective charge capacity. The electrochemical reaction was further studied by applying XRD to the selected discharged and charged states, as shown in Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0003">3</a> c and d. At state B (discharged to 0.2 V), the new diffraction peaks match well with a cubic phase of USn<sub>3</sub>.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0015_R_d30742899e677" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Physica B+C">15</a></span> The simulated XRD pattern of NaSn<sub>3</sub> is identical to the experimental results in Figure S13 (in the Supporting Information).<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0005c" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0005c_R_d30742899e681" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5c - Adv. Mater.">5c</a></span> After discharge to 0.1 V (state C), a tetragonal NaSn with a space group of <i>I</i>4<sub>1</sub>/<i>acd</i> can be observed. Upon discharging to 0.06 V (state D), the characteristic diffraction peaks of Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> are evident, and after full discharge to 0.01 V, the diffraction peaks at 16.5°, 19.0°, 31.8°, 33.3°, 34.7°, and 37.4° indicate the final discharge product of cubic Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cssc201801662-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/acdaaf94-9c96-4fa8-99f6-9e7024ee14e1/cssc201801662-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/acdaaf94-9c96-4fa8-99f6-9e7024ee14e1/cssc201801662-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1dc94815-5410-48de-bfc4-4f4081c89c9f/cssc201801662-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/acdaaf94-9c96-4fa8-99f6-9e7024ee14e1/cssc201801662-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc201801662-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.201801662" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) CV curves at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>; (b) GITT profiles; (c) XRD patterns of the Sn/Cu anode at different states during the first cycle (d); and (e) refined crystal structures of the intermediates NaSn<sub>3</sub>, NaSn, Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>, and Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> in a discharging process.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Rietveld refinement was performed to illustrate the phase transitions. The XRD patterns of Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> are refined to give reasonably small <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> factors of &lt;10 %, which implies reliability of the refined results, as shown in Figures S14 and S15 (in the Supporting Information). The detailed structure parameters are summarized in Tables S1 and S2 (in the Supporting Information). As depicted in Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0003">3</a> e and Table S3 (in the Supporting Information), with Na<sup>+</sup> inserted into Sn, it is transformed to cubic NaSn<sub>3</sub> and then orthorhombic NaSn, the crystal structure of which is obtained from reference data.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0005c" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0005c_R_d30742899e754" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5c - Adv. Mater.">5c</a></span> Upon further sodiation, the rhombohedral Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> is formed, which contains Sn–Sn dimers with a distance of 2.85 Å and five Na atoms oriented in the same direction. Finally, cubic Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> is generated after full discharge, in which an isolated Sn atom is surrounded by Na atoms in relatively symmetric 12-fold coordination, with neighboring distances of three Na atoms at approximately 3.24 Å, six Na atoms at around 3.40–3.42 Å, and another three Na atoms at about 3.55 Å. The volume change is around 425.7 %, the effect of which is efficiently minimized by the 3D porous structured Sn/Cu. In the reverse process, Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> is gradually desodiated to Sn with four distinctive plateaus of states E–I in Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0003">3</a> c. Of note, the characteristic diffraction peak of Cu<sub>6</sub>Sn<sub>5</sub> is not changed during the cycles, which is indicative of nonactivity of Cu<sub>6</sub>Sn<sub>5</sub> in SIBs, as reported.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0016_R_d30742899e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16">16</a></span> However, the Cu<sub>6</sub>Sn<sub>5</sub> acts as a buffer layer to improve the electronic conductivity between active Sn and Cu foil. It is speculated that the reversible reactions of Sn in cycles are Sn⇄NaSn<sub>3</sub>⇄NaSn⇄Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>⇄Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>.</p>
         
         <p>As a benefit of the 3D porous nanostructure of Sn/Cu (Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0001">1</a>), it presents superior rate performance (Figure S16 in the Supporting Information). The discharge/charge profiles of the fourth cycle at different current densities are displayed in Figure S17 (in the Supporting Information). It delivers reversible capacities of 823, 759.6, 735, 712.3, 705.5, 688.7, and 667.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities from 0.2 to 0.5, 1, 2, 3, 5, and 6.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The good capacity retention and low polarization at high current densities indicate outstanding cycle performance and rate capability. These originate from the 3D porous Sn structure of Sn/Cu, which benefits the accommodation of large volume change during sodiation and desodiation and favors fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics and good electric conductivity. The long cycle stability of Sn/Cu is examined at 2.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> in Figures S18 and S19 (in the Supporting Information). Remarkably, after 400 cycles, it still presents a capacity of 659.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and high capacity retention of 94.6 %. In addition, the morphology of the Sn/Cu electrode after 100 cycles is captured by SEM in Figure S20, in which the porous nanostructure and integrity of Sn are well maintained. Among the reported Sn anodes in Table S4 (in the Supporting Information), the electrochemical performance of the porous Sn/Cu electrode is outstanding, which is ascribed to its integrated 3D porous structure.</p>
         
         <p>A full SIB with a configuration of Sn/Cu//Na<sub>3</sub>(VO<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>F<sub>2</sub> (Sn/Cu//NVPF) was constructed (Figure S21 in the Supporting Information). The synthesized NVPF cathode displays reversible cathodic and anodic peaks, and delivers a stable discharge/charge capacity of approximately 108 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> with long cycle stability (Figures S22 and S23 in the Supporting Information).<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0017_R_d30742899e833" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17">17</a></span> In the typical discharge/charge curves of the full battery in Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0004">4</a> a, reversible capacities are achieved for 85.4, 76.7, 71.8, 59.1, 52, and 48.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at varied current densities of 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 0.8, and 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, based on the total mass of cathode and anode materials (the capacity ratio of anode and cathode is 1.25). In comparison with reported full SIBs, the Sn/Cu//NVPF battery exhibits both higher energy (311.7 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>) and power density (182.5 W kg<sup>−1</sup>; Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0004">4</a> b).<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0018_R_d30742899e848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18">18</a></span> Moreover, good cycling performance over 200 cycles is achieved without significant degradation (Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0004">4</a> c), which is attributed to the fast Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics and structural stability. The flexibility of the integrated 3D porous Sn/Cu was tested in a pouch cell, as shown in Figure <a href="#cssc201801662-fig-0004">4</a> d and Figure S24 (in the Supporting Information). This bendable pouch cell can be twisted around a wrist and power a “NKU” logo by a series of light emitting diodes (LEDs). These suggest promising application of the integrated 3D porous Sn/Cu anode for SIBs.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cssc201801662-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/25a91570-864c-49a6-807e-f69772f5b7a2/cssc201801662-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/25a91570-864c-49a6-807e-f69772f5b7a2/cssc201801662-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/082cbc6f-9640-46e1-8a75-02e522b9e363/cssc201801662-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/25a91570-864c-49a6-807e-f69772f5b7a2/cssc201801662-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc201801662-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.201801662" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Typical discharge/charge profiles of the Sn/Cu//NVPF full battery at different current densities. (b) Comparison of the average discharge voltage and specific capacity of the full battery with those reported: <span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0018a" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0018a_R_d30742899e877" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18a - Adv. Mater.">18a</a></span> Hard carbon//O<sub>3</sub>-Na<sub>0.9</sub>[Cu<sub>0.22</sub>Fe<sub>0.30</sub>Mn<sub>0.48</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>; <span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0018b" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0018b_R_d30742899e892" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18b - Adv. Mater.">18b</a></span> Sb//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F; <span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0018c" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0018c_R_d30742899e905" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18c - ACS Energy Lett.">18c</a></span> Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb/Cu//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>; and <span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0018d" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0018d_R_d30742899e918" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18d - J. Power Sources">18d</a></span> graphite//Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub>. (c) Cycle performance of the full battery. (d) Demonstration of a pouch-type full battery driving a LED pattern.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In summary, an integrated 3D porous Sn/Cu electrode was successfully prepared by a facile replacement reaction between Cu and Sn<sup>2+</sup> in the presence of a chelating agent of thiourea and a surfactant of SDS. The synthesized Sn/Cu electrode exhibits a highly reversible capacity of 823.0 and 667.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 and 6.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, and a capacity of 659.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with capacity retention of 94.6 % after 400 cycles. This superior electrochemical performance is attributed to the integrated 3D porous Sn/Cu electrode. The full battery of Sn/Cu//NVPF was assembled, which presented a capacity of 108 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> and an energy density of 311.7 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, which is higher than the state-of-the-art SIBs. The facile strategy for the integrated 3D porous Sn/Cu electrode will pave a new way for development of advanced materials and low-cost SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc201801662-sec-0002">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc201801662-sec-0002-title"> Experimental Section</h3>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc201801662-sec-0003">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="cssc201801662-sec-0003-title"> Chemicals</h4>
               
               <p>Cu foil (99 %, Qingyuan metal material Co. Ltd.), tin sulfate (SnSO<sub>4</sub>, 99 %, Aladdin), thiourea (CH<sub>4</sub>N<sub>2</sub>S, 99.0 %, Macklin), polyvinylpyrrolidone K30 ((C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>9</sub>NO)<sub><i>n</i></sub>, PVP-k30, Chengdu Kelong), sodium dodecyl benzene sulfonate (C<sub>18</sub>H<sub>29</sub>NaO<sub>3</sub>S, 90.0 %, SDBS, Tianjin Guangfu), hexadecyl trimethyl ammonium bromide (C<sub>19</sub>H<sub>42</sub>BrN, CTAB, Tianjin Guangfu), sodium dodecyl sulfate (C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>25</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>Na, SDS, Tianjin Guangfu), vanadium(V) oxide (V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, 99 %, Energy Chemical), oxalic acid dihydrate (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⋅2 H<sub>2</sub>O, 99.5 %, Aladdin), ammonium phosphate monobasic (NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, 99 %, Aladdin), and sodium fluoride (NaF, 98 %, Aladdin) were of analytical grade.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc201801662-sec-0004">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="cssc201801662-sec-0004-title"> Synthesis of the Sn/Cu</h4>
               
               <p>Cu foil was immersed in dilute sulfuric acid for 30 min and subsequently washed with deionized water and ethanol several times to remove surface impurities before use. SnSO<sub>4</sub> (20 mmol) was dissolved in H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> aqueous solution (200 mL, 0.85 <span class="smallCaps">m)</span>, and then thiourea (10.0 g) and SDS (0.2 g) were dissolved into the above solution under stirring. The clean Cu foil was selectively covered by nail enamel on one side, then it was immersed into the above solution for different reaction times (1, 3, 5 h) under stirring at room temperature. The treated Cu foil was washed with ethanol, ethyl acetate (removing the nail enamel), and deionized water several times. The final product was dried at 50 °C for 10 h under vacuum. For comparison, the Sn/Cu samples were prepared with different concentrations of Sn<sup>2+</sup> and different surfactants of SDBS, CTAB, and PVP at different reaction times.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc201801662-sec-0005">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="cssc201801662-sec-0005-title"> Na<sub>3</sub>(VO<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>F<sub>2</sub> (NVPF)</h4>
               
               <p>NVPF was synthesized by a solid-state reaction according to the previous report.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0017_R_d30742899e1048" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17">17</a></span> Typically, V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (2 mmol) and H<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><b>⋅</b>2 H<sub>2</sub>O (6 mmol) were dissolved in distilled water (30 mL) under stirring at 80 °C. After the solution became transparent, NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> (6 mmol) was added. The solution was dried at 80 °C overnight. The dried mixture was ground in an agate mortar and then transferred into a tube furnace with Ar-fluxing at 400 °C for 3 h and at 650 °C for 6 h. The obtained precursor was mixed with NaF, and calcined at 600 °C for 2 h in an Ar atmosphere to produce NVPF.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc201801662-sec-0006">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="cssc201801662-sec-0006-title"> Material characterization</h4>
               
               <p>The pristine Cu foil, NVPF, and Sn/Cu were characterized at a scan rate of 4° min<sup>−1</sup> from 10° to 80° with XRD (Rigaku MiniFlex600 X-ray generator, CuK<sub>α</sub> radiation, <i>λ</i>=1.5406 Å). The morphology, microstructure, and EDS elemental mappings were obtained with SEM (JEOL, JSM7500F), AFM (Bruker Multimode 8), and TEM (FEI, Talos F200X). The FIB sample was prepared with Helios NanoLab 460HP (FEI). The composition of Sn in Sn/Cu electrode was estimated by inductively coupled plasma (ICP: PerkinElmer Optima 8300, Germany).</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc201801662-sec-0007">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="cssc201801662-sec-0007-title"> Electrochemical measurements</h4>
               
               <p>The Sn/Cu was directly used as an electrode without adding any conductive agents or polymer binders. The NVPF cathode was prepared in air by spreading a slurry of NVPF (70 wt %), Super P carbon (20 wt %), and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF; 10 wt %) onto aluminum foil. The electrochemical performance was evaluated in CR2032 coin cells at room temperature, which were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox (Mikrouna Universal 2440/750) with water and oxygen contents below 1 ppm. The average mass loading of Sn (reaction for 3 h) on Cu foil is around 1.2 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> according to ICP measurements. In the half cells, sodium foil was used as the anode. Glass microfiber filters (16 mm in diameter, GF/F: 0.7 μm, Whatman) were used as the separator. The electrolyte was composed of 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> (Sigma–Aldrich, 99 %) in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DEGDME, Meryer, 99.5 %). Additionally, 60 μL of electrolyte was used in each coin cell. The electrochemical performance was measured at different current densities with a LAND battery-test instrument (CT2001A). CVs were conducted using a Solartron 1470E (AMETEK). Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS: Zahner IM6ex) was obtained by applying a sine wave with an amplitude of 5.0 mV over the frequency range from 100 mHz to 100 kHz. The electrode was disassembled from coin cells and rinsed with DME for SEM and XRD analyses. The Sn/Cu//NVPF full battery was constructed by using NVPF as the cathode and Sn/Cu as the anode in a CR2032 coin cell. The capacity ratio of Sn/Cu to NVPF was controlled to be 1.25. The operation voltage windows of the Na//Sn/Cu half cell, Na//NVPF half cell, and Sn/Cu//NVPF full battery were fixed at 0.01–1.0 V, 2.0–4.5 V, and 3.0–4.3 V, respectively.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc201801662-sec-0008">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="cssc201801662-sec-0008-title"> Computational methods</h4>
               
               <p>DFT calculations were performed by using the B3LYP hybrid density functional implemented in the Gaussian 09 suite of program.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0019_R_d30742899e1110" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19">19</a></span> The geometry was optimized at the B3LYP/6-31G** theoretical level and the single-point energy was calculated at the B3LYP/6–311+G** theoretical level, and water was considered as the solvent within the SMD solvent model.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0020_R_d30742899e1112" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Phys. Chem. B">20</a></span> Vibrational frequency calculations were carried out to obtain the thermal correction to Gibbs free energy and confirm no negative frequencies in the optimized structure. Atomic dipole corrected Hirshfeld population (ADCH) charge and the contour line map of charge density difference were obtained from the open source Multiwfn package,<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0021_R_d30742899e1114" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Comput. Chem.">21</a></span> by using the output file obtained from Gaussian 09 calculations. To get the optimized NaSn<sub>3</sub> structure, we carried out DFT calculations by using the Vienna ab initio simulation package (VASP).<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0022_R_d30742899e1118" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Comput. Mater. Sci.">22</a></span> Projector-augmented wave (PAW) pseudopotentials were adopted with an energy cutoff of 450 eV.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0023_R_d30742899e1121" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Phys. Rev. B">23</a></span> The Heyd–Scuseria–Ernzerhof (HSE) screened hybrid functional was applied to geometry optimization.<span><a href="#cssc201801662-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201801662-bib-0024_R_d30742899e1123" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Chem. Phys.">24</a></span> All of the structural parameters were optimized until all force components were less than 0.02 eV Å<sup>−1</sup> and the convergence criterion of the total energy was 10<sup>−5</sup> eV. The NaSn<sub>3</sub> was modeled by replacing the U atoms of USn<sub>3</sub> with Na atoms.</p>
               </section>
            </section>
         
         <div class="article-section__content">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc201801662-sec-0009-title"> Acknowledgements</h3>
